  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_b24e685c-6c1b-48d0-b741-58039e09e933'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };

      initializeSiwa();

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer17993279263242136295"><p>Four thousand years ago, a stranger's death at the Old Temple of Ratharryn-and his ominous "gift" of gold-precipitates the building of what for centuries to come will be known as one of mankind's most singular and remarkable achievements. Bernard Cornwell's epic novel <i>Stonehenge</i> catapults us into a powerful and vibrant world of ritual and sacrifice at once timeless and who</p></span>
  <span id="freeText17993279263242136295" style="display:none"><p>Four thousand years ago, a stranger's death at the Old Temple of Ratharryn-and his ominous "gift" of gold-precipitates the building of what for centuries to come will be known as one of mankind's most singular and remarkable achievements. Bernard Cornwell's epic novel <i>Stonehenge</i> catapults us into a powerful and vibrant world of ritual and sacrifice at once timeless and wholly original-a tale of patricide, betrayal, and murder; of bloody brotherly rivalry: and of the never-ending quest for power, wealth, and spiritual fulfillment.<br /></p><p><br />Three brothers-deadly rivals-are uneasily united in their quest to create a temple to their gods. There is Lengar, the eldest, a ruthless warrior intent on replacing his father as chief of the tribe of Ratharryn; Camaban, his bastard brother, a sorcerer whose religious fervor inspires the plan for Stonehenge; and Saban, the youngest, through whose expertise the temple will finally be completed. Divided by blood but united-precariously-by a shared vision, the brothers begin erecting their mighty ring of granite, aligning towering stones to the movement of the heavenly bodies, and raising arches to appease and unite their gods. Caught between the zealousness of his ambitious brothers, Saban becomes the true leader of his people, a peacemaker who will live to see the temple built in the name of salvation and regeneration.<br /></p><p><br />Bernard Cornwell, long admired for his rousing narrative and meticulous historical imaginings, has here delivered his masterpiece, the most compelling and powerful human drama of its kind since Ken Follett's <i>Pillars of the Earth</i> and Edward Rutherford's <i>Sarum</i>. His re-creation of civilization as it might have been in 2000 B.C. at once amplifies the mystery of his subject and makes the world of Stonehenge come alive as never before.<br /></p></span>
  <a data-text-id="17993279263242136295" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

<div id="review_47827255" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/47827255" />
    <a title="Terri" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1968826-terri"><img alt="Terri" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1356560426p2/1968826.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/47827255?book_show_action=true">Feb 28, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Terri" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Terri" href="/user/show/1968826-terri">Terri</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780006513865" href="/book/show/871151.Stonehenge">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1968826-terri?shelf=britain-fiction">britain-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1968826-terri?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1968826-terri?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer47827255" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6633802576499350328">It really has me baffled that some people don't like this book. I found it enthralling and captivating.<br/>There was something about Bernard Cornwell's version of these bronze age people and their mystical and monolithic Stonehenge, that captured my imagination and I felt stirred by both them and their pristine, unpolluted environment.<br/>They were innocent and gullible, ignorant and sweet, yes, even at their deadliest or maddest. They are unblemished by a modern world. Their existence is aligned in eve</span>
  <span id="freeText6633802576499350328" style="display:none">It really has me baffled that some people don't like this book. I found it enthralling and captivating.<br />There was something about Bernard Cornwell's version of these bronze age people and their mystical and monolithic Stonehenge, that captured my imagination and I felt stirred by both them and their pristine, unpolluted environment.<br />They were innocent and gullible, ignorant and sweet, yes, even at their deadliest or maddest. They are unblemished by a modern world. Their existence is aligned in every way with nature and the elements. Everything was an omen or an augury. If a bird lit from a tree, they watched it to see where it headed, if a swan lifts from the waterway into the sky, they stop to watch it's direction in hopes of anticipating the future. They wear 'sea monsters' teeth on sinew around their necks, and dress their ring ditches with animal and human skulls to ward off people and spirits alike. They are a deep and cerebral people.<br />While this life may sound restrictive to you and I, everything has a meaning and a meaning in everything, I think it was beautiful to read about and I felt more connected to pre history than I have ever been before. <br />And all this due to the wizened hand of a master author? <br />I had some trepidation going into this book because of the mixed reviews on Goodreads, but I should have known Cornwell would not let me down, he hasn't yet after all, why should he now?<br />Reading this book was an experience for me and I wish I had not put it off for as long as I had.<br />Thankyou Bernard Cornwell.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6633802576499350328" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1415467918" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Artemas" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6751111-artemas"><img alt="Artemas" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1452871375p2/6751111.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1415467918?book_show_action=true">Oct 13, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Artemas" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Artemas" href="/user/show/6751111-artemas">Artemas</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780061091940" href="/book/show/540636.Stonehenge">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6751111-artemas?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6751111-artemas?shelf=prehistoric-fiction">prehistoric-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1415467918" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9417834857355407213">The key to being a successful historical fiction author is the ability to open a window into the place and era in which you write. Bernard Cornwell does this seamlessly. Cornwell tells a completely imagined story revolving around mystical Stonehenge that made me believe the events could have actually taken place in the distant past. Within the “Historical Notes” section at the back of the book, he even states that the names for the gods and goddesses were completely made up, yet everything felt </span>
  <span id="freeText9417834857355407213" style="display:none">The key to being a successful historical fiction author is the ability to open a window into the place and era in which you write. Bernard Cornwell does this seamlessly. Cornwell tells a completely imagined story revolving around mystical Stonehenge that made me believe the events could have actually taken place in the distant past. Within the “Historical Notes” section at the back of the book, he even states that the names for the gods and goddesses were completely made up, yet everything felt authentic.<br /><br />What a story! Though I rated this one 5 stars, I will say that the story builds slowly, but the pay-off at the end was worth it. Much of this book details how pre-Bronze Age people actually managed to move and shape the massive stones that made Stonehenge … so if that kind of detail bores you, then this one probably isn’t for you ... in which case I'd recommend you go kick some rocks. Though it dealt with a completely different era and story, <i>Stonehenge</i> reminded me of Ken Follett’s <i>Pillars of the Earth</i>, which I also loved. If you enjoyed Pillars, then I would bet that you would enjoy this as well.<br /><br /><i>Stonehenge</i> was an interesting glimpse into a sparsely covered period of historical fiction. Like the gods upon the great temple’s completion, I am pleased with this momentous effort.<br /><br />Get your copy here: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://amzn.to/2wo43iW">https://amzn.to/2wo43iW</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="9417834857355407213" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText15249523720761765331" style="display:none">Cornwell catches us up in a fascinating page turning historical novel with science fantasy elements, in this elecrtic epic set in the Britain of 4000 years ago.<br />Cornwell creates an eventful, vivid, gory, gripping and spellbinding tale of love and loss, sorcery, hatred, jealousy, greed, ambition and pagan theology.<br /><br />Traces the story of three brothers, the evil and savage killer, Lengar, the cunning cripple turned sorcerer, Camaban, who ruthlessly sheds blood to build a new stone temple that will usher in new age free of suffering and death-the ruthless idealism of causes massive death and suffering for a utopian ideal, that was a hallmark of the twentieth century CE, and Saban, an intelligent but somewhat naive third brother who is caught in the machinations of his malignant older brothers.<br /><br />Derewynn, who is the bride of Saban who is then raped and enslaved by Lengar and his friends, before becoming a formidable sorceress and chieftainess, and Aurenna the priestess and second bride of Saban, to be the disciple and high priestess of Camaban.<br />One thing that was absent that would have been helpful would have been a map showing where the places named are today in modern Britain.<br />Ultimately about the author's story of how Stonehenge came to be in his imagination. Another page-turning Cornwell historical thriller, which you will want to add to your collection.</span>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/111836196?book_show_action=true">Jul 15, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ron" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ron" href="/user/show/1256262-ron">Ron</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer111836196" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3533364388489692240">A good story, well-written, but not up to what I expect from Bernard Cornwell. He builds on John North's <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1371312.Stonehenge_Neolithic_Man_and_the_Cosmos' title='Stonehenge Neolithic Man and the Cosmos by John North' rel='nofollow noopener'>Stonehenge: Neolithic Man and the Cosmos</a> to include a plausible explanation of what little we know about this wonder of the ancient world, contemporaneous with the Great Pyramids of Egypt.<br/><br/>But the book doesn't connect. For one thing it lacks the humor which leavens the drama and gore of his Wessex series. (I assume Sharpe has his humorous moments, too.) I never grew to care for Saban as I shou</span>
  <span id="freeText3533364388489692240" style="display:none">A good story, well-written, but not up to what I expect from Bernard Cornwell. He builds on John North's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1371312.Stonehenge_Neolithic_Man_and_the_Cosmos" title="Stonehenge Neolithic Man and the Cosmos by John North" rel="nofollow noopener">Stonehenge: Neolithic Man and the Cosmos</a> to include a plausible explanation of what little we know about this wonder of the ancient world, contemporaneous with the Great Pyramids of Egypt.<br /><br />But the book doesn't connect. For one thing it lacks the humor which leavens the drama and gore of his Wessex series. (I assume Sharpe has his humorous moments, too.) I never grew to care for Saban as I should for a flawed hero.<br /><br />Also, the rate of action seems totally beyond what an early Bronze Age community should be capable. The biggest oak they can find is cut down in a day? With stone, flint and bronze tools?<br /><br />Still, so far as I know, there are no historical howlers, and it was a good read. Had a lessor author written it, I might have rated it with four stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3533364388489692240" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_878978368" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/878978368" />
    <a title="Victor Bruneski" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/29425183-victor-bruneski"><img alt="Victor Bruneski" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442987549p2/29425183.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/878978368?book_show_action=true">Mar 11, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Victor Bruneski" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Victor Bruneski" href="/user/show/29425183-victor-bruneski">Victor Bruneski</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer878978368" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14265510239732870849">Bernard Cornwell is one of my favorite authors, and I have been looking forward to reading this for some time. But with all that I am still surprised on how good the book is.<br/><br/>This story is a bit different from the usual Cornwell yarn. From the books I have read by him, it is apparent he likes to write about war, and is probably one of the best writers out there in describing battle scenes. There is battles in this book too, but the are few and far between. This is more about primitive man, and th</span>
  <span id="freeText14265510239732870849" style="display:none">Bernard Cornwell is one of my favorite authors, and I have been looking forward to reading this for some time. But with all that I am still surprised on how good the book is.<br /><br />This story is a bit different from the usual Cornwell yarn. From the books I have read by him, it is apparent he likes to write about war, and is probably one of the best writers out there in describing battle scenes. There is battles in this book too, but the are few and far between. This is more about primitive man, and their relationship with their gods.<br /><br />This is about three brothers, and events surrounding them that brings about the creation of Stonehenge. Little is known about Stonehenge, but Cornwell incorporates the littliest things into his story, making it a pleasure to read. <br /><br />This novel really draws you in, making you feel like you are there in the distant past, making you the characters struggles and their hopes in building Stonehenge. I loved the characters in the story. Camadan and Saban really stood out.<br /><br />The story went by to fast, and left me wanting more. Signs of a great novel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14265510239732870849" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_540805876" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/540805876" />
    <a title="Abigail Hartman" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2272332-abigail-hartman"><img alt="Abigail Hartman" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1530881804p2/2272332.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/540805876?book_show_action=true">Feb 19, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Abigail Hartman" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Abigail Hartman" href="/user/show/2272332-abigail-hartman">Abigail Hartman</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780061091940" href="/book/show/540636.Stonehenge">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2272332-abigail-hartman?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer540805876" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17936733620923272153">"It's violent," says Dad.<br/><br/>"Oh," says I, with a lofty wave of the hand, "violence is all right. I can handle THAT."<br/><br/>Turns out, Dad knew what he was talking about. This book was far, far grittier than my usual fare, and there were several times where I had to put it aside and read something as cheery as "Howl's Moving Castle" to settle the emotions. It is not a difficult book stylistically, but the weight of darkness and paganism was so great that it took me over a month to plow through to the end. </span>
  <span id="freeText17936733620923272153" style="display:none">"It's violent," says Dad.<br /><br />"Oh," says I, with a lofty wave of the hand, "violence is all right. I can handle THAT."<br /><br />Turns out, Dad knew what he was talking about. This book was far, far grittier than my usual fare, and there were several times where I had to put it aside and read something as cheery as "Howl's Moving Castle" to settle the emotions. It is not a difficult book stylistically, but the weight of darkness and paganism was so great that it took me over a month to plow through to the end. Cornwell doesn't skimp on the brutal details of life in Britain two thousand years before the birth of Christ, and he certainly doesn't try to present the newfangled idea of innocent, ignorant people who lived in communion with nature. In fact, he directly repudiates such notions in his Historical Note. Archeological evidence shows pretty clearly that the lives of these men and women revolved around death - early deaths, sacrificial deaths, violent deaths - and so does this novel. That hardly makes for a light and cheery read.<br /><br /><br />The novel is also intriguing, however, which was what kept me reading. There are really no "likeable" characters; pitiable, yes, and sometimes even sympathetic, but not likeable or heroic. I certainly didn't keep reading for them. But I wanted to know how it would end, and, perhaps like Saban himself, I wanted to see Stonehenge completed. I also respect his ability to incorporate, or rather weave a story around, historical details and archaeological finds; it was very nice to be able to say, "Oh! Oh! I know that archer's cuff! <i>I got that reference!</i>" That is the best sort of historical fiction.<br /><br />So I got through, and while I can hardly say I'll be reading more of Cornwell's works due to the sheer grittiness of them, I do appreciate his ability to craft an anachronism-free work and to hold the reader to the end.. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17936733620923272153" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_361477848" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/361477848" />
    <a title="Billy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10554432-billy"><img alt="Billy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1591121312p2/10554432.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/361477848?book_show_action=true">Jul 04, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Billy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Billy" href="/user/show/10554432-billy">Billy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer361477848" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3276872980210245592">Ancient architecture 101...,<br/><br/>No matter how you slice it, the book is about how to build a stone temple in ancient times. There is a story behind this, an interesting and complex tale of people from all over present-day England, surrounding three brothers, sons of a chieftan, and their aspirations, plottings, dreams and failures. There is travel throughout the ancient island with Cornwell's requisite description of place and people, but it still comes back to building the stone temple; so the titl</span>
  <span id="freeText3276872980210245592" style="display:none">Ancient architecture 101...,<br /><br />No matter how you slice it, the book is about how to build a stone temple in ancient times. There is a story behind this, an interesting and complex tale of people from all over present-day England, surrounding three brothers, sons of a chieftan, and their aspirations, plottings, dreams and failures. There is travel throughout the ancient island with Cornwell's requisite description of place and people, but it still comes back to building the stone temple; so the title suggests, so the book goes...<br /><br />This is not, in my humble opinion (and I am a big fan of Cornwell), Bernard's best work. It is interesting enough. There is treachery, murder, rivalry, betrayal, lust, insanity, god-worship, slavery, battle, bravery, courage and cowardice, but all offered in less heaping helpings than usually offered from Cornwell.<br /><br />Surely, it was no small task for our neolithic ancestors to collect the stones, shape them, move them and stand them so precisely, but the description of such consumes probably 75-80% of the book. Cornwell does his best to make this description interesting, but I believe that where this book really falls short is in its length. The tale could have been told in fewer pages with fewer people and less outside storylines.<br /><br />The back-story is sufficient, but nothing outstanding. To be honest, I'm a bit surprised by the praise offered by the "experts"; people that get paid to review. I think the experts failed us on this one. This is an average read with Cornwell's exceptional ability to lay out the scene bringing the rating up slightly.<br /><br />This is certainly an interesting read for anyone that is interested in ancient history, but I don't think that the average historical fiction reader will find much to be excited about here. The battles are weak in comparison to Cornwell's usual epic warfare storytelling. There is no real mystery, though the book does not promise this; I have just come to expect this from Cornwell so I offer it as an aside.<br /><br />I am very interested in anything prehistoric/ancient and I did enjoy reading about the tools, processes, people and lifestyles of our forefathers, but I realize that some may not take so much enjoyment from the tale.<br /><br />***I give 4 stars, my personal rating, understanding full well that some may disagree and so also offer the reasons why I believe some may not be quite so interested. Overall, I would rate this a 3+, rounding up to 4 because Cornwell can make even the grinding and standing of stones sound interesting. But some may want to skip this one and move on to a more involving tale.***</span>
  <a data-text-id="3276872980210245592" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_37403145" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/37403145" />
    <a title="Debbie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1698579-debbie"><img alt="Debbie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1239712587p2/1698579.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/37403145?book_show_action=true">Nov 11, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Debbie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Debbie" href="/user/show/1698579-debbie">Debbie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780006513865" href="/book/show/871151.Stonehenge">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1698579-debbie?shelf=historical">historical</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer37403145" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer971393087749042820"><b>TEDIUM SET IN STONE</b><br/><br/>This book was one of about five books that my 14 year old son gave me for my birthday - all sourced from a bargain bin! I have honestly been doing my best to read some of them and have only given two away to the school fair without even opening the covers . And in fact, I actually thought that I might enjoy this one. Unfortunately not.<br/><br/>I have never read any books by Bernard Cornwell before and won't be back for more. The book started out promisingly enough but somewhere around </span>
  <span id="freeText971393087749042820" style="display:none"><b>TEDIUM SET IN STONE</b><br /><br />This book was one of about five books that my 14 year old son gave me for my birthday - all sourced from a bargain bin! I have honestly been doing my best to read some of them and have only given two away to the school fair without even opening the covers . And in fact, I actually thought that I might enjoy this one. Unfortunately not.<br /><br />I have never read any books by Bernard Cornwell before and won't be back for more. The book started out promisingly enough but somewhere around the middle I was bored to tears and forced myself to finish. It did get better again towards the end and my eyes stopped glazing over while reading but that's not saying much.<br /><br />The book tells the story of events that could have led to the building of Stonehenge. Lengar, Camaban and Saban are three brothers whose lives are inextricably bound with the temple. Camaban, the crippled outcast son born with a club foot transforms himself into a powerful sorcerer and designs the massive temple. Saban his younger brother has the engineering skills to move the leviathan stones and make Camaban's design a reality. Lengar the older brother is a bitter rival who tries to destroy Camaban. <br /><br />So all the ingredients are right there for a great story. Greed, ambitition, revenge, rivalry, hatred, betrayal. Add goddesses, religious tension, blood sacrifice and a few beautiful women and this should have been a riveting story. Instead the plot moved as slow and ponderously as the building of Stonehenge itself. The characters remained one dimensional and mostly unlikeable and by midway through the book I really didn't care what happened to any of them. It was slightly interesting to read about the way people may have lived back in 2000BC, but not worth wading through 580 pages for.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="971393087749042820" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1307208081" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1307208081" />
    <a title="Jennifer" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1599338-jennifer"><img alt="Jennifer" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442501106p2/1599338.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1307208081?book_show_action=true">Jun 13, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jennifer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jennifer" href="/user/show/1599338-jennifer">Jennifer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1307208081" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9488943487494361693">I can't say that I enjoyed this book. It has a dry, spare style that feels almost like a list in some places. There are lengthy passages to do with the details of moving the stones that aren't particularly well done or interesting. The characters are hard to identify with, though I'm not of the opinion that you have to identify with characters in order to enjoy a book, it is often helpful. It takes a grim and brutal view of human nature which isn't necessarily a bad thing but presented in this h</span>
  <span id="freeText9488943487494361693" style="display:none">I can't say that I enjoyed this book. It has a dry, spare style that feels almost like a list in some places. There are lengthy passages to do with the details of moving the stones that aren't particularly well done or interesting. The characters are hard to identify with, though I'm not of the opinion that you have to identify with characters in order to enjoy a book, it is often helpful. It takes a grim and brutal view of human nature which isn't necessarily a bad thing but presented in this humorless manner is not particularly enjoyable. <br /><br />The book tries to envision what would drive people to build a structure like Stonehenge. There is madness, self-delusion and many battles of will. While I am interested in what drives people to do crazy/epic/impossible things the way the story was told was not of particular interest to me. The protagonist was the most likable, yet least interesting character who really had no agency and whose life and destiny was in thrall to his stronger-willed brothers. The brothers were more interesting but also horrible. I liked the idea of the crippled brother trying to make the world straight but, again, it was not done in a way that resonated for me. The role of the female characters was problematic as women were second rate yet some had much power. Again, this could have been compelling but not the way it was done here. Perhaps the most interesting part of the book was the author's note at the end. I have no doubt that this was painstakingly researched but the storytelling was somehow lacking. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9488943487494361693" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_102590288" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/102590288" />
    <a title="Bill Shears" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2821519-bill-shears"><img alt="Bill Shears" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1258641689p2/2821519.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/102590288?book_show_action=true">May 13, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bill Shears" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bill Shears" href="/user/show/2821519-bill-shears">Bill Shears</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">engineers, ancient monument fans</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer102590288" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4777885391752130265">Finally plowed all the way through this, for two reasons. One: we wanted to see the darn thing built. No spoiler there. You know the thing does get built.<br/><br/>How they raised the lintels is always a matter of controversy and as a warning for some <i>Chariots of the Gods</i> fans, there are no aliens involved, which is good thing. Give the humans some credit for the emergence of intelligence, and application of brute force which would have been their strength in that era.<br/><br/>It's a plausible-sounding method I ha</span>
  <span id="freeText4777885391752130265" style="display:none">Finally plowed all the way through this, for two reasons. One: we wanted to see the darn thing built. No spoiler there. You know the thing does get built.<br /><br />How they raised the lintels is always a matter of controversy and as a warning for some <i>Chariots of the Gods</i> fans, there are no aliens involved, which is good thing. Give the humans some credit for the emergence of intelligence, and application of brute force which would have been their strength in that era.<br /><br />It's a plausible-sounding method I hadn't heard of as a possibility. It does not involve building earthen ramps as has been proposed, due to -- as one of the characters points out -- a thin top soil layer over the chalk bedrock of the area; there might not have been enough earth to gather for ramps with reasonable effort. We won't give the lintel-lifting method away. If it interests you then you are the reader the author is looking for. <br /><br />The other reason we wanted to see this book through to the end was, without even peeking, we sensed there would be an afterward where the author talks about the research on which the book was based. We were looking forward to that and was not disappointed.<br /> <br />The author's strength is in the knowledge -- no, complete mastery - of the subject exhibited in that Historical Note afterward. He admits at the outset of the Historical Note that nothing of the plot, characters or action are anything but fiction. <br /><br />Limitation of the narrative, in fact, prevents some of the more interesting findings on the site from being included, such as the fact that there are painted circles in the parking lot at Stonehenge that mark the locations of ancient post holes from 8000 BC, 5000 years prior to the start of the Neolithic Stonehenge construction. <br /> <br />In the story, a political/religious conflict involving early Bronze Age villages is conjured up, and all of it struggles to reach the level of contrivance. At the root of it, you find out early, is gold. In the afterward is a mention that a body had been found that had been buried with gold piece artifacts just like those in the story. <br /><br />The hero, Saban, is not all that heroic, letting events wash over him pretty much exactly like a hero isn't supposed to do, and at times, indeed, allowing himself to be pushed around and knocked about. At one point the line "Seize him!" is actually spoken. Enough said.<br /><br />All in all a run-of-the-mill impetus to a plodding build-up to the construction of one of humankind's earliest engineering achievements. Read it for the account of the gathering, transportation and placement of the stones, but don't expect much from the surrounding story.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4777885391752130265" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

I could hardly put this book down, finished within a short time and it's still among my all-time favorites.</span>

<div id="review_159787496" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/159787496" />
    <a title="Jim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1895570-jim"><img alt="Jim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1484874117p2/1895570.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/159787496?book_show_action=true">Apr 08, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jim" href="/user/show/1895570-jim">Jim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1895570-jim?shelf=historical-novels">historical-novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer159787496" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2731538143902315099">After his series of Saxon novels about the England of Alfred the Great, Bernard Cornwell has continued his attack on organized religion. In the Saxon novels, he opposed Christianity to Asatru -- the Viking religion -- in favor of the latter, which could be called a <b>DIS</b>organized religion. With <b>Stonehenge</b>, Cornwell shows us three brothers in a mythical kingdom called Ratharryn: Saban, the builder; Lengar, the cruel warrior; and Camaban, the mad priest of Slaol (sort of sounds like Slay-All, doesn'</span>
  <span id="freeText2731538143902315099" style="display:none">After his series of Saxon novels about the England of Alfred the Great, Bernard Cornwell has continued his attack on organized religion. In the Saxon novels, he opposed Christianity to Asatru -- the Viking religion -- in favor of the latter, which could be called a <b>DIS</b>organized religion. With <b>Stonehenge</b>, Cornwell shows us three brothers in a mythical kingdom called Ratharryn: Saban, the builder; Lengar, the cruel warrior; and Camaban, the mad priest of Slaol (sort of sounds like Slay-All, doesn't it?), the Sun God.<br /><br />As Camaban forces Saban over the years to build the monstrous new temple to Slaol, he becomes progressively crazed and violent, and his expectations of the temple more outlandish:<blockquote>"You still want to be chief?" Saban asked, still dazed by the night's events. "Yes," Camaban said, "Ido. I want other things as well. No more winter, no more sickness, no more children crying in the night. That is what I want." He had come close to Saban as he spoke. "I want union with the gods," he went on softly, "and endless summer."</blockquote>It's safe to say that England has never yet enjoyed an endless summer, though, with Global Warming, who knows?<br /><br />In my reading, Cornwell supplies my need for adventure porn. Having read all the Sharpe and Saxon novels, I see recurring motifs, many of them connected with the love interests, who seem to come and go. But then, the point could be made that long happy marriages were not a regular feature of life in past times, what with endless tribal wars or Viking raids or even the mass dislocations caused by something like the Napoleonic Wars. <br /><br />There are still several Cornwell series I have yet to start. If I had world enough and time, I will continue to do so.<br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2731538143902315099" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Virginia Owl" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/864373-virginia-owl"><img alt="Virginia Owl" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1201898696p2/864373.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Virginia Owl" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Virginia Owl" href="/user/show/864373-virginia-owl">Virginia Owl</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/864373-virginia-owl?shelf=historic-fiction">historic-fiction</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer14286566" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer643862343740745846">Great for folks who wonder HOW Stonehenge was build. Not so good on the WHY of it all.<br/><br/>I listened to this book on tape. It took me a long time to get through it. My overall impression of the book is that it was OK, but definitely in the "borrow it from the library" category, NOT the "go out and buy it NOW" category.<br/>The sense of place and time was strong & to my ear, realistic. But I found the details involved in the building of Stonehenge became tedious and overwhelming (though this could be more</span>
  <span id="freeText643862343740745846" style="display:none">Great for folks who wonder HOW Stonehenge was build. Not so good on the WHY of it all.<br /><br />I listened to this book on tape. It took me a long time to get through it. My overall impression of the book is that it was OK, but definitely in the "borrow it from the library" category, NOT the "go out and buy it NOW" category.<br />The sense of place and time was strong &amp; to my ear, realistic. But I found the details involved in the building of Stonehenge became tedious and overwhelming (though this could be more pronounced in the audio version- you could skim over the slow bits in print, harder to do in audio)<br />I liked the day to day details of the characters lives but struggled with Cornwell's version of the characters religion/motivation: he vacillates between treating it with respect &amp; subtly mocking it from a modern perspective. (Spoiler alert coming up) The author's basic premise is that Stonehenge was built draw the lunar year into sync with the solar one. As a pagan &amp; someone who has a passionate interest in European pre-history, that motivation seems patronizing and well, stupid. I could go on about this at length, but i'll leave it at that.<br />I found the characters in this book very two dimensional- lots of stereotypical good &amp; bad guys. And don't get me started on his female characters- I've never seen them as a strong suit in any of the Cornwell books i've read.<br /><br />Basic summary: a cool book for flavor, time &amp; place and great for anyone who wonders "HOW did they build that". But the author's answer for WHY Stonehenge was build didn't work at all for this reader. </span>
  <a data-text-id="643862343740745846" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review14286566">

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/11468654-stonehenge">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26760286-margo?shelf=amazon-kindle">amazon-kindle</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26760286-margo?shelf=audio-cds">audio-cds</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2016694646" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17645126899418772027"><br/><br/>I enjoyed this story a lot more than I had expected to. It was my first <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/12542.Bernard_Cornwell' title='Bernard Cornwell' rel='nofollow noopener'>Bernard Cornwell</a> but definitely won't be my last. The narration was by Sean Barrett and initially I found his voice slow and monotonous but I warned to his by the end of the first disc. His dinner tones have an air of gravitas to the reading that was quite appropriate.<br/><br/>The story was set 4000 years ago in the Neolithic age and the author did a wonderful job of describing the way out ancestors lived, their values and their supe</span>
  <span id="freeText17645126899418772027" style="display:none"><br /><br />I enjoyed this story a lot more than I had expected to. It was my first <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/12542.Bernard_Cornwell" title="Bernard Cornwell" rel="nofollow noopener">Bernard Cornwell</a> but definitely won't be my last. The narration was by Sean Barrett and initially I found his voice slow and monotonous but I warned to his by the end of the first disc. His dinner tones have an air of gravitas to the reading that was quite appropriate.<br /><br />The story was set 4000 years ago in the Neolithic age and the author did a wonderful job of describing the way out ancestors lived, their values and their superstitions. It was a great remaining of how Stonehenge might have been built, the enormous amount of manpower and inventiveness that was involved in the undertaking.<br /><br />I would describe this as an historical adventurer. It explores the mythology of the era - I would say imagined on the basis on the archaeological evidence at the site itself combined with what little of the druidic religion. It was very atmospheric.</span>

<div id="review_810308" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/810308" />
    <a title="Patrick Trisler" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/64531-patrick-trisler"><img alt="Patrick Trisler" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1198188643p2/64531.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/810308?book_show_action=true">Apr 20, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Patrick Trisler" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Patrick Trisler" href="/user/show/64531-patrick-trisler">Patrick Trisler</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer810308" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14615882192489809899">Boo. A bad book. The subject of Stonehenge has enough mystery as to why it was built and by whom to allow anybody to take a stab at it. Its all theoretical, so why not make something up thats interesting. Well, here it is not that interesting. The basic facts about Stonehenge are much more interesting than the drama presented here. This book was what would have happened if Stonehenge was built as the set of a soap opera. The characters are thin, and seem to be guessing as to what is going on aro</span>
  <span id="freeText14615882192489809899" style="display:none">Boo. A bad book. The subject of Stonehenge has enough mystery as to why it was built and by whom to allow anybody to take a stab at it. Its all theoretical, so why not make something up thats interesting. Well, here it is not that interesting. The basic facts about Stonehenge are much more interesting than the drama presented here. This book was what would have happened if Stonehenge was built as the set of a soap opera. The characters are thin, and seem to be guessing as to what is going on around them. If you are going to set people in a cold, savage world where human sacrifice is part of day to day life, you have got to make those people relatable in some way. And I don't know where this dude came up with the names for the characters and there gods, but they sounded like something out of a bad fantasy game. Of all the names that early man had for the sun god, you don't need to go making one up as bad as Slaol. Some historical context, please! Read 'Hengeworld' by Mike Pitt before you read this jive.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14615882192489809899" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer545878467" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1562000356646725095">Good solid storytelling based on an interesting theory of why Stonehenge was built. Of course we don't know why, but archaeology has given us insight into how Stonehenge was built and Cornwell does a credible job building a world in which this extraordinary labor could have taken place. Vivid details and compelling character round out the action and plot.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer10563237215713237403"><b>DNF 50% ish</b><br/><br/>I understand that women have been treated shitty throughout history but this book took it to a whole new level.   <span id="freeText10563237215713237403" style="display:none"><b>DNF 50% ish</b><br /><br />I understand that women have been treated shitty throughout history but this book took it to a whole new level. The one strong female character is killed off early and the rest are described as cattle, heavily sexualised and only measured by the men they marry/give birth to. Only male personas are explored and explained, even then they are dense as shit. Female characters are delicate, subserviant, mysterious and up for the taking. The primal misogynistic viewpoint the author has used to animate this ancient culture is distant from the more gender-equal society that pre-Roman Britain is supposed to have been. It is honestly pages of selfish, power-hungry men fighting, raping, killing, eating and, absurdly, still being dull and predictable.<br /><br />Conclusion: a load of monotonous pish that makes you angry.</span>

<div id="review_2277032410" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2277032410" />
    <a title="Jonathan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8258265-jonathan"><img alt="Jonathan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1590662168p2/8258265.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2277032410?book_show_action=true">Feb 08, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jonathan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jonathan" href="/user/show/8258265-jonathan">Jonathan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780007550890" href="/book/show/23018853-stonehenge">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8258265-jonathan?shelf=historical-novels">historical-novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2277032410" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8339742217962240742">The story itself, about three very different brothers who are involved in the building of the temple that we know as Stonehenge, I enjoyed. As we don't really know who built it, or why, Bernard Cornwell was able to create a story which needed to sound at least credible. This he did, and made it exciting, quite bloodthirsty, and involving a large cast of characters. Some seemed a bit formulaic, but overall they represented types which haven't changed much over time (power driven; bullies; religio</span>
  <span id="freeText8339742217962240742" style="display:none">The story itself, about three very different brothers who are involved in the building of the temple that we know as Stonehenge, I enjoyed. As we don't really know who built it, or why, Bernard Cornwell was able to create a story which needed to sound at least credible. This he did, and made it exciting, quite bloodthirsty, and involving a large cast of characters. Some seemed a bit formulaic, but overall they represented types which haven't changed much over time (power driven; bullies; religious zealots; deep thinkers; heroes and villains etc.). Most of the women have a rotten time, but that's nothing new (or old!), and there was a suggestion that the only strong female characters got respect by being a bit mad (and therefore scary). For anyone who enjoys historical fiction it is a good, well-imagined story with an air of 'could have been so' about it. </span>
  <a data-text-id="8339742217962240742" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review2277032410">

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('l+akqaCrdnaVIF6KO2FPq+4ZnFDHuPwa4A4XY9j+FagW3rtIlTnBASd8QoNgkqOOebTnYonitoePyU2oPs6Iog==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

  }

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/12542.Bernard_Cornwell">About Bernard Cornwell</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/12542.Bernard_Cornwell"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1504578807p3/12542.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/12542.Bernard_Cornwell">Bernard Cornwell
</a></div>
<div class='goodreadsAuthorBadge'></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
13,026
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer8935296060997944224">Cornwell was born in London in 1944. His father was a Canadian airman, and his mother, who was English, a member of the Women's Auxiliary Air Force. He was adopted and brought up in Essex by the Wiggins family, who were members of the Peculiar People, a strict Protestant sect who banned frivolity of all kinds and even medicine. After he left them, he changed his name to his birth mother's maiden n</span>
  <span id="freeText8935296060997944224" style="display:none">Cornwell was born in London in 1944. His father was a Canadian airman, and his mother, who was English, a member of the Women's Auxiliary Air Force. He was adopted and brought up in Essex by the Wiggins family, who were members of the Peculiar People, a strict Protestant sect who banned frivolity of all kinds and even medicine. After he left them, he changed his name to his birth mother's maiden name, Cornwell.<br /><br />Cornwell was sent away to Monkton Combe School, attended the University of London, and after graduating, worked as a teacher. He attempted to enlist in the British armed services at least three times but was rejected on the grounds of myopia.<br /><br />He then joined BBC's Nationwide and was promoted to become head of current affairs at BBC Northern Ireland. He then joined Thames Television as editor of Thames News. He relocated to the United States in 1980 after marrying an American. Unable to get a green card, he started writing novels, as this did not require a work permit.<br /><br />As a child, Cornwell loved the novels of C.S. Forester, chronicling the adventures of fictional British naval officer Horatio Hornblower during the Napoleonic Wars, and was surprised to find there were no such novels following Lord Wellington's campaign on land. Motivated by the need to support himself in the U.S. through writing, Cornwell decided to write such a series. He named his chief protagonist Richard Sharpe, a rifleman involved in most major battles of the Peninsular War.<br /><br />Cornwell wanted to start the series with the Siege of Badajoz but decided instead to start with a couple of "warm-up" novels. These were <i>Sharpe's Eagle</i> and <i>Sharpe's Gold</i>, both published in 1981. <i>Sharpe's Eagle</i> was picked up by a publisher, and Cornwell got a three-book deal. He went on to tell the story of Badajoz in his third Sharpe novel, <i>Sharpe's Company</i>, published in 1982.<br /><br />Cornwell and wife Judy co-wrote a series of novels, published under the pseudonym "Susannah Kells". These were <i>A Crowning Mercy</i>, published in 1983, <i>Fallen Angels</i> in 1984, and <i>Coat of Arms</i> (aka <i>The Aristocrats</i>) in 1986. (Cornwell's strict Protestant upbringing informed the background of <i>A Crowning Mercy</i>, which took place during the English Civil War.) In 1987, he also published <i>Redcoat</i>, an American Revolutionary War novel set in Philadelphia during its 1777 occupation by the British.<br /><br />After publishing eight books in his ongoing Sharpe series, Cornwell was approached by a production company interested in adapting them for television. The producers asked him to write a prequel to give them a starting point to the series. They also requested that the story feature a large role for Spanish characters to secure co-funding from Spain. The result was <i>Sharpe’s Rifles</i>, published in 1987, and a series of Sharpe television films staring Sean Bean.<br /><br />A series of contemporary thrillers with sailing as a background and common themes followed: <i>Wildtrack</i> published in 1988, <i>Sea Lord</i> (aka <i>Killer's Wake</i>) in 1989, <i>Crackdown</i> in 1990, <i>Stormchild</i> in 1991, and <i>Scoundrel</i>, a political thriller, in 1992.<br /><br />In June 2006, Cornwell was made an Officer of the Order of the British Empire in the Queen's 80th Birthday Honours List.<br /><br />Cornwell's latest work, <i>Azincourt</i>, was released in the UK in October 2008. The protagonist is an archer who participates in the Battle of Agincourt, another devastating defeat suffered by the French in the Hundred Years War. However, Cornwell has stated that it will not be about Thomas of Hookton from The Grail Quest or any of his relatives.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8935296060997944224" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
